[0:0:400,335] Natural  

[1:0:379,366] Resources  

[2:0:344,409] Natural Resources Agency programs protect and  

[2:1:344,418] restore California's diverse natural and cultural  

[2:2:344,428] resources for current and future generations,  

[3:0:344,438] including operating state parks, managing water  

[3:1:344,448] supplies, supporting forest health, encouraging  

[3:2:344,458] clean energy, and preserving fish and wildlife  

[3:3:344,468] habitats, rich farmlands and mineral resources.  

[3:4:344,478] Agency programs also protect the public through  

[3:5:344,488] construction of levees in the Central Valley and  

[4:0:344,498] suppression of wildfires. These programs not only  

[4:1:344,508] contribute to the state's unique quality of life, but  

[4:2:344,517] also are critical to sustaining a vibrant economy.

[1:0:242,53] 3100   Exposition Park  

[2:0:66,75] The Office of Exposition Park Management, the Science Center, and the California African American Museum (CAAM), are  

[2:1:66,85] located in Exposition Park, a 160-acre tract in south Los Angeles, which is owned by the state. For budget purposes, these  

[2:2:66,95] three departments are collectively known as Exposition Park.  

[3:0:66,113] The Office of Exposition Park Management provides long-term leadership in the development and implementation of park  

[3:1:66,123] usage policy and day-to-day management, operation and promotion of the park for its tenants and the public. The mission of  

[3:2:66,133] the Science Center is to stimulate curiosity and inspire science related learning. The mission of CAAM is to research, collect,  

[3:3:66,143] preserve, and interpret, for public enrichment, the history, art, and culture of African Americans with an emphasis on California  

[3:4:66,154] and the western United States.  

[4:0:66,171] Because   the Science Center's   programs drive a need for infrastructure investment, the   Science Center   has a capital outlay  

[4:1:66,182] program to support this need. For the specifics on the Science Center's capital outlay program see "Infrastructure Overview."

[5:0:66,304] PROGRAM DESCRIPTIONS  

[6:0:66,323] 2300 - CALIFORNIA SCIENCE CENTER  

[7:0:66,344] Attracting over 2 million guests annually, the California Science Center represents one of California's premier educational and  

[7:1:66,354] family destinations. Governed by a nine-member Board of Directors appointed by the Governor, the Science Center develops  

[7:2:66,364] and features award-winning exhibits and internationally renowned education programs.  

[8:0:66,384] Hands-on educational exhibits and programs focus on science, math, technology, and conservation which explore biological  

[8:1:66,395] processes of humans, animals, plants, the Earth's ecosystems, habitats, and geophysical processes, as well as engineering,  

[8:2:66,405] communications, and transportation, on land and in space. In 2012, the Space Shuttle Endeavour was moved to its temporary  

[8:3:66,415] home - the Samuel Oschin Pavilion - while design and construction of the Science Center's Air and Space Center - its  

[8:4:66,425] permanent home - is completed.  

[9:0:66,446] In addition, the Science Center operates a 3D IMAX theater which features science related films and documentaries. The  

[9:1:66,456] Center for Science Learning offers professional development programs to improve math and science skills of teachers and  

[9:2:66,466] other educators. The Science Center School is a K-5 science, math and technology focused neighborhood charter school. As a  

[9:3:66,476] Title I school, it serves one of the more underserved and economically challenged communities in South Los Angeles. The  

[9:4:66,486] school's instructional programs and teacher training meet California science standards.  

[10:0:66,507] The Science Center's mission values accessibility and inclusiveness and strives to inspire interest in science among those  

[10:1:66,517] traditionally underrepresented in science, math, technology and engineering. The Science Center receives significant funding  

[10:2:66,527] support for exhibit development and capital outlay projects from the California Science Center Foundation.  

[11:0:66,545] 2305 - EXPOSITION PARK MANAGEMENT  

[12:0:66,563] The Office of Exposition Park Management (OEPM) is responsible for public safety operations, parking services management  

[12:1:66,573] and overall park beautification maintenance. In addition, OEPM administers supervision and coordination of park-wide events,  

[12:2:66,583] and facilitates communication efforts between state, city, county, and private entities represented within the park. OEPM is  

[12:3:66,593] responsible for the negotiations of ground leases and agreements which impact the state's interest, including development of  

[12:4:66,603] park facilities and future infrastructure improvements. OEPM is also responsible for managing the Exposition Park  

[12:5:66,614] Improvement Fund and generating revenue to maintain the park. Exposition Park enriches the internal park community and in  

[12:6:66,624] addition provides green space and recreational areas for the surrounding neighborhood.  

[13:0:66,642] 2310 - CALIFORNIA AFRICAN AMERICAN MUSEUM  

[14:0:66,659] The California African American Museum (CAAM) is governed by a seven-member board of directors appointed by the  

[14:1:66,669] Governor. CAAM’s mission is to research, collect, preserve, and interpret, for public enrichment, the history, art, and culture of  

[14:2:66,680] African Americans with an emphasis on California and the western United States. Founded in 1977, as the first African  

[14:3:66,690] American museum of art, history, and culture fully supported by a state, CAAM was the direct result of a sustained, multiyear  

[14:4:66,700] campaign of activism undertaken by visionary founders and community members. CAAM occupies a 44,000-square-foot facility  

[14:5:66,710] in Exposition Park, which includes 15,000 square feet of gallery space and an additional 13,000 square feet of space for public  

[15:0:66,736] * Dollars in thousands, except in Salary Range. Numbers may not add or match to other statements due to rounding of budget details.

[2:0:207,29] 3100   Exposition Park - Continued 

[3:0:66,83] programming. The museum’s permanent collection houses 4,000 objects that span paintings, photographs, film, sculpture,  

[3:1:66,93] historical documents, and artifacts. CAAM has a strong mandate to support the work of under-represented artists of color and  

[3:2:66,103] to think expansively about the way that people of the African diaspora have contributed to American culture and the world at  

[3:3:66,113] large. That is achieved through a rotating exhibition schedule and public programming for visitors of all ages. More than forty  

[3:4:66,124] years after its founding, the originating principle for CAAM remains vital to a healthy and just America--to create and support an  

[3:5:66,134] inclusive, accessible, and dynamic space where all people can see black lives, culture, and experiences valued and reflected.

[7:1:66,379] 2320 - TAHOE REGIONAL PLANNING AGENCY  

[8:0:66,399] A congressionally approved compact between California and Nevada established the Tahoe Regional Planning Agency. The  

[8:1:66,409] Agency coordinates planning and regulations that preserve and enhance the environment and resources of the Lake Tahoe  

[8:2:66,420] Basin. Funding for the Agency is shared between Nevada (one-third) and California (two-thirds).  

[8:3:66,437] 2325 - YOSEMITE FOUNDATION PROGRAM  

[9:0:66,458] This program receives revenue from the sale of personalized motor vehicle license plates by the Department of Motor Vehicles.  

[9:1:66,468] The funds are allocated by the State Controller for restoration and preservation projects in Yosemite National Park.  

[9:2:66,486] 2330 - SEA GRANT PROGRAM  

[10:0:66,506] The Sea Grant Program encourages research and education in the fields of marine resources and technology. This program  

[10:1:66,516] provides funding to the University of California and University of Southern California to match federal funds for selected  

[10:2:66,526] projects under the federal Sea Grant Program.

[6:0:201,273] 3125   California Tahoe Conservancy  

[7:0:66,294] The California Tahoe Conservancy's mission is to lead California's efforts to restore and enhance the natural and recreational  

[7:1:66,305] resources of the Lake Tahoe Basin (Basin). The Conservancy achieves its mission by developing and implementing programs  

[7:2:66,315] that acquire environmentally sensitive land; provide grants or directly fund soil erosion prevention and watershed restoration;  

[7:3:66,325] and improve stream environments, wildlife habitats, forest health, and water quality. The Conservancy balances these activities  

[7:4:66,335] with programs to improve public access and recreational opportunities in an environmentally sound manner by acquiring  

[7:5:66,345] lakefront property, constructing pedestrian and bike paths, and building public access facilities.  

[8:0:66,366] The Conservancy is also a key implementing agency of the Environmental Improvement Program (EIP), which is intended to  

[8:1:66,376] restore and protect the Basin's natural resources.   The EIP leverages funding from the states of California and Nevada, the  

[8:2:66,386] Tahoe Regional Planning Agency, the federal government, local governments, and various private entities for projects needed  

[8:3:66,396] to achieve environmental goals in the Basin. The EIP is updated periodically to include more refined estimates of projects,  

[8:4:66,407] modifications in the scope of identified projects, and inclusion of new projects. 

[9:0:66,427] Because the Conservancy's programs drive a need for infrastructure investment, the Conservancy has a capital outlay program  

[9:1:66,437] to support this need. For the specifics on the Conservancy's capital outlay program see "Infrastructure Overview."

[2:0:166,29] 3125   California Tahoe Conservancy - Continued 

[3:0:66,83] PROGRAM DESCRIPTIONS  

[3:1:66,102] 2340 - TAHOE CONSERVANCY  

[4:0:66,120] The Conservancy owns and manages nearly 4,700 properties totaling more than 6,500 acres, including thousands of quarter-

[4:1:66,130] acre lots within the Basin’s urban areas. These parcels provide open space, water quality and recreational benefits, and have  

[4:2:66,140] significantly reduced the potential level of development in the Basin. The Conservancy manages its lands for multiple benefits  

[4:3:66,151] and inspects each parcel once every two years to monitor forest health and to identify maintenance needs and encroachments.  

[4:4:66,161] Maintaining healthy forests in urban areas to help protect life and property constitutes another fundamental role of the  

[4:5:66,171] Conservancy’s land management program. The Conservancy also manages 11 properties with recreational amenities,  

[4:6:66,181] including six beaches that provide public access to Lake Tahoe.  

[5:0:66,199] The Conservancy and its partners are increasingly shifting to large landscape, multi-jurisdictional, multiple-benefit projects to  

[5:1:66,209] more rapidly and comprehensively achieve restoration goals in the Basin. The Conservancy plans to continue working with a  

[5:2:66,219] wide variety of partners to restore the health and resilience of the Basin’s forests and watersheds, with the aim of also  

[5:3:66,230] increasing the pace and scale of restoration. This includes improving forest diversity, protecting wildlife, applying beneficial fire,  

[5:4:66,240] clearing meadows of encroaching trees, reducing streambank erosion, reconnecting floodplains, and treating storm water. This  

[5:5:66,250] work will enhance the ability of these lands to respond to wildfire, drought, insects, and climate change.  

[6:0:66,268] The Conservancy also works closely with local governments to meet its mission by providing grants for acquisitions and site  

[6:1:66,278] improvements such as building roadside and storm drainage facilities and sediment basins, building bike trails, and creating  

[6:2:66,288] public gathering spaces and improving public access to the Lake.  

[6:3:66,306] 2345 - ACQUISITION AND SITE IMPROVEMENT PROJECTS  

[7:0:66,326] This program acquires environmentally sensitive lands and provides public access and recreation by enhancing existing  

[7:1:66,336] facilities and establishing new public access sites along the Lake and in natural areas. The program also preserves a wide  

[7:2:66,347] variety of habitats supporting endangered, threatened or rare animal and plant species, and restores streams and watersheds  

[7:3:66,357] such as marshes, meadows and riparian areas to mitigate development damage and enhance water quality.  

[8:0:66,374] In addition, the program is helping to reduce greenhouse gas emissions and achieve environmental benefits by promoting  

[8:1:66,385] urban in-fill development in the Basin. These efforts include selling, leasing, or exchanging non-environmentally sensitive  

[8:2:66,395] Conservancy lands and associated development rights to support revitalization efforts consistent with local area plans and town  

[8:3:66,405] center objectives.   Property exchanges and the proceeds from the sale or leasing of property are then used to (1) acquire other  

[8:4:66,415] environmentally sensitive lands or (2) fund conservation and other projects that support the Conservancy in fulfilling its mission.

[11:0:188,530] 3210   Environmental Protection Program  

[12:0:66,552] The California Environmental Protection Program receives revenue from the sale of personalized motor vehicle license plates  

[12:1:66,562] by the Department of Motor Vehicles. Revenues are deposited in the California Environmental License Plate Fund. Funds  

[12:2:66,572] expended pursuant to Division 13.5 of the Public Resources Code must have one or more of the following purposes:  

[12:3:65,590] • The control and abatement of air pollution, including all phases of research into the sources, dynamics and effects of  

[12:4:76,600] environmental pollutants.

[5:0:66,244] PROGRAM DESCRIPTIONS  

[5:1:66,264] 2360 - TRAINING AND WORK PROGRAM  

[5:2:66,281] This Program focuses on four areas:  

[6:0:66,299] Natural Resource Work: Corpsmembers protect and enhance the state's natural resources through park development, trail  

[6:1:66,309] construction, tree planting, fire hazard reduction, watershed improvement, wildlife habitat enhancement, removal of non-native  

[6:2:66,320] vegetation, meadow restoration, energy and water auditing and retrofitting, irrigation system installation, and drought-tolerant  

[6:3:66,330] and other landscaping.  

[7:0:66,347] Disaster Response: Corpsmembers are dispatched to fires, assisting with initial attack, mop-up and logistical support; floods,  

[7:1:66,358] filling sandbags, reinforcing levees and stabilizing hillsides; earthquakes, removing hazards and staffing disaster assistance  

[7:2:66,368] centers; oil spill clean-up; snow removal; search and rescue operations; pest infestation eradication; and homeland security  

[7:3:66,378] assistance.  

[8:0:66,396] Corpsmember Education: Corpsmembers are provided opportunities to advance their academic skills while in the CCC through  

[8:1:66,406] local adult and charter schools and community colleges. 

[9:0:66,424] Corpsmember Development and Training: The CCC stresses the development of both a work and service ethic, which includes  

[9:1:66,434] teamwork, self-discipline, leadership, and giving back to California. Corpsmembers learn conservation principles and career  

[9:2:66,444] planning. The CCC also offers training in trail building, first aid, Hazardous Waste Operations and Emergency Response, and  

[9:3:66,454] firefighting certification, which can lead to internship opportunities with various employers in California.

[8:0:66,480] INFRASTRUCTURE OVERVIEW  

[9:0:66,499] The California Conservation Corps (CCC) operates 26 facilities statewide, including 9 residential and 17 non-residential  

[9:1:66,509] facilities in both urban and rural areas. These facilities enable the CCC to achieve its mission of providing employment, training,  

[9:2:66,520] and educational opportunities to young men and women. The facilities also allow the CCC to assist federal, state and local  

[9:3:66,530] agencies, as well as nonprofit entities, with the conservation of California's natural resources.  

[9:4:66,548] The CCC residential facilities house 80 to 100 corpsmembers each and operate 24 hours a day, bringing young people  

[9:5:66,558] together from across the state, and increasing capacity for emergency response and conservation projects. Non-residential  

[9:6:66,568] centers range in size from 30 to 60 corpsmembers and allow young people with families to live at home and report to work.

[5:0:105,214] 3360   Energy Resources Conservation and Development Commission  

[6:0:66,235] The Energy Resources Conservation and Development Commission (Energy Commission) is responsible for ensuring a  

[7:0:66,245] reliable supply of energy to meet state needs while protecting public health, safety, and the environment. Activities include the  

[7:1:66,256] following: making public policy recommendations regarding energy; collecting targeted energy data and ensuring data is  

[7:2:66,266] managed responsibly; developing and implementing research, development, demonstration, and deployment policies and  

[7:3:66,276] programs that promote strategic energy investments; ensuring energy facilities approved by the Energy Commission are  

[8:0:66,286] designed, constructed, operated, and decommissioned in a manner that protects the environment and public health and safety,  

[8:1:66,296] and in compliance with all applicable laws, ordinances, regulations, and standards; adopting building   efficiency standards, and  

[8:2:66,307] adopting and enforcing appliance efficiency standards; promoting development and deployment of advanced transportation  

[8:3:66,317] technology; and supporting climate change goals through integrated resource planning, renewable energy development, and  

[8:4:66,327] system integration.

[16:0:66,437] MAJOR PROGRAM CHANGES  

[17:0:65,456] • Building Initiative for Low-Emissions Development Program – The Budget includes $80 million Building Initiative for Low-

[17:1:76,466] Emissions Development (BUILD) Program funds over three years, beginning with $40 million in the budget year, to provide  

[17:2:76,477] incentives for the installation of near-zero greenhouse gas emission technologies such as heat pumps, induction cooking,  

[17:3:76,487] and other traditionally gas appliances and building fixtures in new, low-income residential housing as one measure to reduce  

[17:4:76,497] greenhouse gas emissions and decarbonize California’s building sector. Of the $80 million, $60 million will provide direct  

[17:5:76,507] incentives ($30 million in budget year); $10 million will provide funding for technical assistance and market education and  

[17:6:76,517] outreach ($5 million in budget year); $8 million will provide total support costs to administer the BUILD Program ($4 million in  

[17:7:76,528] budget year); and $2 million is allocated for joint evaluation cost share ($1 million in budget year).

[5:0:66,398] PROGRAM DESCRIPTIONS  

[6:0:66,418] 2380 - REGULATORY and PLANNING PROGRAM  

[7:0:66,445] The Regulatory and Planning program facilitates markets in providing adequate statewide energy supplies. The program  

[7:1:66,456] certifies power plant sites, develops and implements landscape-scale plans to identify suitable areas for renewable energy  

[7:2:66,466] resources and infrastructure development, designates transmission line corridors, and monitors new energy facilities and fuel  

[7:3:66,476] infrastructure for compliance and supply and distribution adequacy consistent with state energy policies. The program develops  

[7:4:66,486] state energy policy based on the economic, security, safety, and environmental land-use and climate implications of energy  

[7:5:66,496] supply and demand. The Energy Assessments program develops long-range projections for future energy supplies and  

[8:0:66,507] demand, maintains current information on statewide electrical generation sources, consumption and peak demand, natural gas,  

[8:1:66,517] transportation fuels, and distributed generation. The program develops state energy policy based on the economic, financial,  

[8:2:66,527] security, safety, and environmental implications of supply, demand, and price/cost assessments, and monitors energy markets  

[8:3:66,537] for competitive prices and potential market abuses.  

[9:0:66,555] 2380010 - Power Plant Site Certification and Transmission Line Corridor Designation Program 

[10:0:66,573] The Power Plant Site Certification and Transmission Line Corridor Designation programs are responsible for the following:  

[10:1:66,583] reviewing thermal power plant siting applications 50 megawatts and larger; ensuring power plant developers comply with  

[10:2:66,593] conditions of certification and all applicable laws and regulations when constructing, operating and closing power plants, and  

[10:3:66,603] investigating potential violations; reviewing petitions to amend siting decisions; reviewing applications for transmission line  

[10:4:66,613] corridor designation; developing environmental and land use information and evaluating constraints and opportunities for  

[10:5:66,624] renewable energy infrastructure development; and analyzing safety, reliability, efficiency, resilience, and environmental  

[10:6:66,634] performance issues related to power plants and the state electricity transmission grid.  

[10:7:66,652] 2380019 - Electricity Analysis  

[11:0:66,669] The Electricity Analysis program is responsible for evaluating current and future electricity supply market trends and  

[11:1:66,679] infrastructure needs; estimating costs and emissions of various generation types; assessing the adequacy of electricity  

[11:2:66,690] supplies; and analyzing regulations and environmental issues related to electricity. Additionally, this program addresses the  

[11:3:66,700] distribution system and its relationship to distributed generation, including planning and interconnection barriers and provides  

[11:4:66,710] information and recommendations to state agencies, electricity market participants, and the public. The natural gas program

[3:0:66,83] component is responsible for evaluating current and future natural gas market trends and infrastructure needs, assessing the

[4:0:66,159] 2385 - ENERGY RESOURCES CONSERVATION PROGRAM  

[5:0:66,187] The Energy Resources Conservation program objectives include reducing overall energy use and decreasing peak electricity  

[5:1:66,197] demand by identifying energy efficiency opportunities and developing and implementing programs and standards for efficiently  

[5:2:66,207] using energy in all sectors.  

[6:0:66,225] 2385010 - Building and Appliances Program  

[7:0:66,253] The Building and Appliances program objectives include reducing consumer and business energy use, water use, and peak  

[7:1:66,263] demand by developing and implementing strategies and minimum efficiency standards for appliances, equipment, and new and  

[7:2:66,273] existing residential and nonresidential buildings. The Building and Appliances program provides technical assistance on  

[7:3:66,284] building standards and enforcement support to the building industry and building departments. Further, the program maintains  

[7:4:66,294] a database of efficient appliances and equipment, and enforces compliance with the appliance efficiency standards.  

[8:0:66,311] 2385019 - Energy Projects Evaluation and Assistance Program  

[9:0:66,339] This program is responsible for providing technical assistance, grants, and loans to improve energy efficiency and reduce  

[9:1:66,350] operating costs of schools, colleges, local jurisdictions, hospitals, and public care facilities.  

[10:0:66,367] 2385028 - Demand Analysis  

[11:0:66,395] The Demand Analysis program is responsible for collecting and analyzing electricity and natural gas consumption data used to  

[11:1:66,405] prepare energy demand forecasts, forecasting peak and total energy consumption by sector, and estimating the amount of  

[11:2:66,416] energy conserved by existing and proposed program activities. The program conducts detailed electricity and natural gas end-

[11:3:66,426] use surveys which serve as a primary data source for energy demand forecasts, conservation potential studies, and market  

[11:4:66,436] and policy impact assessments. The program also performs transportation energy demand forecasting, conducts consumer  

[11:5:66,446] vehicle choice surveys, and assesses the impacts of transportation electrification on California electricity use.  

[12:0:66,482] 2390 - DEVELOPMENT PROGRAM  

[13:0:66,499] The Development Program focuses on transportation and alternatives to conventional fossil fuels. The mission is to support  

[13:1:66,509] provision of adequate and reliable transportation energy to the California transportation sector while balancing economic, public  

[13:2:66,520] health, safety, and environmental consequences. The program also conducts research, development, demonstration,  

[13:3:66,530] commercialization, and deployment activities on energy efficiency, renewable and advanced energy technologies, and  

[13:4:66,540] alternative and renewable fuel and advanced vehicle technologies so that future energy supplies are cost effective, secure, and  

[13:5:66,550] reliable; enhance environmental quality; and promote state and local economic development. The program provides technical  

[13:6:66,560] assistance, financial assistance, direct technology research and demonstration, technology forecasting, technology analysis  

[13:7:66,571] and evaluation, and information transfer.  

[14:0:66,588] 2390010 - Transportation Technology and Fuels  

[15:0:66,616] The Transportation Technology and Fuels program provides financial incentives to develop and deploy innovative technologies  

[15:1:66,626] and strategies that transform California's fuel and vehicle types to help attain the state's climate change policies. The program  

[15:2:66,637] goal is the development and deployment of low-carbon alternative fuels and advanced vehicle technologies in the marketplace,  

[15:3:66,647] without adopting any one preferred fuel or technology. The program provides analyses and recommendations to guide state  

[15:4:66,657] energy policy and legislation to achieve climate change and air quality objectives, reduce petroleum consumption, and promote  

[15:5:66,667] economic development. The program also provides incentives to workforce development and training that intersect with the  

[15:6:66,677] respective program goals.

[7:0:66,483] PROGRAM DESCRIPTIONS  

[8:0:66,503] 2420 - GEOLOGIC HAZARDS AND MINERAL RESOURCES CONSERVATION  

[9:0:66,520] This program evaluates, assesses and maps the state's geologic and seismologic hazards, such as earthquakes, landslides,  

[9:1:66,531] tsunami and volcanic eruption threats, and hazardous minerals exposures, in order to protect the public health and safety and  

[9:2:66,541] the natural environment; analyzes the state's mineral assets and maps its mineral resources. Information is used by federal,  

[9:3:66,551] state, and local government agencies, industries and individual businesses, and the public to make informed decisions about  

[9:4:66,561] land use, seismic safety, and mineral development.  

[9:5:66,579] 2425 - GEOLOGIC ENERGY MANAGEMENT DIVISION  

[10:0:66,597] This program regulates the drilling, operation, and abandonment of oil, natural gas, and geothermal wells to prevent, as   much  

[10:1:66,607] as possible, damage to life, health, property, and natural resources. The program seeks to protect public health and safety and  

[10:2:66,617] environmental quality, including reduction and mitigation of greenhouse gas emissions associated with the development of  

[10:3:66,627] hydrocarbon and geothermal resources in a manner that meets the energy needs of the state. The state is fully reimbursed for  

[10:4:66,637] program expenditures by annual assessments and fees on the respective industries. 

[10:5:66,655] 2430 - LAND RESOURCE PROTECTION  

[11:0:66,673] This program protects agricultural farmland and open space through various financial incentives. The Williamson Act program  

[11:1:66,683] provides advice on and reviews documents related to changes to Williamson Act contracts between landowners and local  

[11:2:66,693] governments. The California Farmland Conservancy Program provides grants to local governments and nonprofit land trusts  

[11:3:66,703] for planning purposes and for the acquisition of agricultural conservation easements that permanently remove development  

[11:4:66,714] rights, and therefore development pressure, from agricultural lands. The Farmland Mapping and Monitoring Program develops  

[12:0:66,736] * Dollars in thousands, except in Salary Range. Numbers may not add or match to other statements due to rounding of budget details.

[3:0:66,83] maps, statistics, and reports relating to farmland conversion, farmland inventory, and land protection to assist in local land use  

[3:1:66,93] decisions.  

[3:2:66,111] 2435 - MINE RECLAMATION  

[4:0:66,128] This program regulates surface mining operations and monitors local lead agencies to ensure compliance with the Surface  

[4:1:66,139] Mining and Reclamation Act of 1975. It assists cities, counties, state agencies, and mine operators in their efforts to reclaim  

[4:2:66,149] mined lands to beneficial uses. This program also compiles an inventory of the state's estimated 47,000 abandoned mines and  

[4:3:66,159] remediates abandoned mine hazards to protect public safety.  

[4:4:66,177] 2440 - STATE MINING AND GEOLOGY BOARD  

[5:0:66,194] The Board serves as a regulatory and policy body for the state's geology, geologic and seismologic hazards, conservation of  

[5:1:66,205] mineral resources, and reclamation of mined lands. The department's California Geological Survey and the Division of Mine  

[5:2:66,215] Reclamation provide the Board with relevant engineering, technical expertise, and support functions for certain reports, plans,  

[5:3:66,225] and maps. The Board also serves as an appeals body for mining operations that have been issued notice of violation orders to  

[5:4:66,235] comply, or administrative penalties and in cases where the Division of Mine Reclamation contests the adequacy of a local  

[5:5:66,245] government’s approval of a mine operation’s financial assurance cost estimate.

[6:0:164,286] 3540   Department of Forestry and Fire Protection  

[7:0:66,307] The California Department of Forestry and Fire Protection (CAL FIRE) serves and safeguards the people and protects the  

[7:1:66,317] property and resources of California.  

[8:0:66,338] CAL FIRE provides all hazard—fire, medical, rescue and disaster—emergency response to the public and provides leadership  

[8:1:66,348] in the protection of life, property, and natural resources.  

[9:0:66,368] CAL FIRE safeguards the public through: engineering; research, development and adoption of regulations; fire and life safety  

[9:1:66,379] programs; fire prevention, law enforcement, and public information and education; resource protection; and emergency  

[9:2:66,389] response.  

[10:0:66,409] CAL FIRE limits the damage caused by fires, disasters, environmental degradation, and related emergencies by employing  

[10:1:66,419] diverse yet complementary efforts, including: training California's fire service professionals, public education and prevention  

[10:2:66,430] awareness, responsible stewardship of our natural resources, and natural resource and emergency management.  

[11:0:66,450] CAL FIRE cultivates mutually beneficial and cost-effective relationships with all levels of government and the private sector to  

[11:1:66,460] enhance the efficiency and effectiveness of public safety programs and mitigate emergencies in the urban, rural, and wildland  

[11:2:66,470] areas of California.  

[12:0:66,491] Because CAL FIRE's programs drive a need for infrastructure investment, CAL FIRE has a capital outlay program to support  

[12:1:66,501] this need. For the specifics on the CAL FIRE's capital outlay program see "Infrastructure Overview."

[6:0:66,212] MAJOR PROGRAM CHANGES  

[6:1:65,231] • CAL FIRE Relief Staffing and Additional Surge Capacity—$85.6 million General Fund ongoing for additional firefighting  

[6:2:76,241] resources to provide CAL FIRE with operational flexibility throughout the peak fire season and beyond as fire conditions  

[6:3:76,251] dictate. These resources will be leveraged to provide relief for frontline firefighting staff, which will directly benefit employee  

[6:4:76,262] health and wellness. Additionally, these resources will serve as an immediate resource pool to be deployed strategically,  

[6:5:76,272] based on fire risk, to build CAL FIRE’s surge capacity by staffing additional engines during the late fall, winter, and early  

[6:6:76,282] spring, and adding a fourth firefighter on a portion of engines.  

[6:7:65,298] • Innovation Procurement Sprint—$4.4 million General Fund ($7.6 million ongoing) to enable CAL FIRE to implement the new,  

[6:8:76,308] pioneering wildfire prediction and modeling technology that was procured through the Innovation Procurement Sprint process  

[6:9:76,319] initiated through Executive Order N-04-19. The recently-executed contract will enable CAL FIRE to access a wildfire  

[6:10:76,329] predictive software program that   will be used to inform fire pre-positioning and suppression tactical operations, with the intent  

[6:11:76,339] to more readily control and contain wildfires, and to protect people and assets at risk.

[6:0:66,432] PROGRAM DESCRIPTIONS  

[6:1:66,452] 2461 - STATE FIRE MARSHAL  

[7:0:66,472] The Office of the State Fire Marshal is responsible for the statewide protection of life and property through the development  

[7:1:66,482] and application of fire protection engineering, education and enforcement.  

[7:2:66,500] 2461010 - Office of the State Fire Marshal (OSFM):  

[8:0:65,518] • Code Development and Analysis: Objectives include the development and adoption of codes relating to fire/life safety used  

[8:1:76,528] statewide by local fire and building authorities. The Code Development and Analysis Division fosters, promotes, and  

[8:2:76,538] develops ways and means of protecting life and property against fire in many ways, including the adoption and  

[8:3:76,548] implementation of regulations for statewide application. This division prepares the OSFM fire and life safety regulations and  

[8:4:76,558] building standards for review and adoption into the California Code of Regulations, Titles 19 and 24. The division assists with  

[8:5:76,569] the application of state laws, regulations, and code enforcement by local fire departments, fire districts, and building  

[8:6:76,579] departments. 

[9:0:65,595] • Fire and Life Safety: This program is the main entity within the department for the application of laws and regulations related  

[9:1:76,605] to fire prevention and life safety. The OSFM is responsible for fire and life safety for state-owned and specified state-

[9:2:76,615] occupied facilities. The Fire and Life Safety Division meets its mission through conducting code compliance inspections of  

[9:3:76,626] new and existing buildings and plan review and construction inspections of all projects on state-owned and specified state-

[9:4:76,636] occupied properties. 

[10:0:65,652] • Fire Engineering and Investigations: This division administers licensing programs and provides services for product  

[10:1:76,662] evaluation, certifications and listings. This division also oversees the Arson and Bomb Unit, which has the responsibility to  

[10:2:76,672] dispose of seized illegal fireworks and conduct fire/explosion investigations and licensing enforcement. The division is  

[10:3:76,683] responsible for 11 statewide programs, which include: Building Materials Listing, Portable Fire Extinguishers, Flame  

[10:4:76,693] Retardant Chemicals and Fabrics, Vapor Recovery, Automatic Fire Extinguishing Systems, Fire Safe Cigarettes, Licensing  

[10:5:76,703] Enforcement and Inspection, Lab Accreditation, Motion Picture and Entertainment, Arson and Bomb Investigation, and  

[11:0:66,736] * Dollars in thousands, except in Salary Range. Numbers may not add or match to other statements due to rounding of budget details.

[0:0:66,13] 2020-21 STATE BUDGET — RES   70 

[1:0:454,13] NATURAL RESOURCES 

[2:0:129,29] 3540   Department of Forestry and Fire Protection - Continued 

[3:0:76,83] Fireworks Disposal. This division's primary functions are licensing, product approval, fire/explosion investigations and  

[3:1:76,93] licensing enforcement.  

[4:0:65,109] • Pipeline Safety: This program oversees the safe construction, operation and maintenance of approximately 6,500 miles of  

[4:1:76,119] intrastate hazardous liquid pipelines that transport crude oil, refined products (e.g., gasoline, diesel, jet fuel, etc.), and Highly  

[4:2:76,130] Volatile Liquids (HVL) throughout California, including, but not limited to, populated urban areas, ecologically sensitive areas,  

[4:3:76,140] and other high consequence areas. Pipelines that fall under the Pipeline Safety Division authority are pipelines that transport  

[4:4:76,150] hazardous liquids between facilities, such as offshore platforms, production fields, refineries, storage terminals, and marine  

[4:5:76,160] terminals. The OSFM regulated pipelines do not include those within production fields, marine terminals, or refineries.  

[5:0:65,176] • State Fire Training: This program administers the California Fire Service Training and Education System and the Fire Service  

[5:1:76,187] Training and Education Program for federal, state and local firefighters. The program oversees a California Fire Academy  

[5:2:76,197] System, which consists of over 50 training academies that are administered through partnerships between fire departments,  

[5:3:76,207] community colleges, and the OSFM. The State Fire Training Program administers a professional certification system for  

[5:4:76,217] firefighters and chief officers and receives program guidance from the State Board of Fire Services.  

[6:0:66,235] 2461019 - California Underground Facilities Safe Excavation Board:  

[7:0:66,253] The Dig Safe Board was created by the passage of Chapter 809, Statutes of 2016 (SB 661) to work on issues related to the  

[7:1:66,263] state's "Call Before You Dig" law, which requires excavators and buried infrastructure owners to coordinate to prevent  

[7:2:66,273] infrastructure damage and unsafe outcomes. The Dig Safe Board is charged with coordinating the state’s safe excavation  

[7:3:66,283] outreach and education efforts, developing safe excavation standards, investigating accidents, and working with partner state  

[7:4:66,293] agencies to enforce the “Call Before You Dig” law.  

[8:0:66,311] 2465 - FIRE PROTECTION  

[9:0:66,332] CAL FIRE provides fire protection services to help limit damages to life, property, and natural resources. The objective is to  

[9:1:66,342] quickly and aggressively attack fires in areas where CAL FIRE has assumed primary direct protection responsibility by virtue of  

[9:2:66,352] law, contract, or agreement until the fire is under control. The level of initial attack and follow-up action is relative to values  

[9:3:66,362] threatened and control difficulty, with the goal to control all unwanted fires under CAL FIRE's jurisdiction within the first  

[9:4:66,372] operational period.  

[10:0:66,390] 2465010 - Fire Prevention:  

[11:0:66,410] This program is responsible for implementation of the Strategic Fire Plan, fire engineering, law enforcement, fire suppression  

[11:1:66,421] cost recovery, and fire prevention education. This program focuses on the most effective methods, materials, and procedures to  

[11:2:66,431] mitigate hazards and to enforce pertinent laws that deter and mitigate fires. More specifically, efforts focus on what needs to be  

[11:3:66,441] done before a wildland fire starts in order to reduce the costs of firefighting, property loss, injury to the public and firefighters,  

[11:4:66,451] and damage to the environment.  

[12:0:66,469] 2465019 - Fire Control:  

[13:0:66,489] The objective of this program is to detect, respond to, and suppress wildland fires in or threatening the State Responsibility  

[13:1:66,500] Area using an aggressive initial attack strategy. CAL FIRE's goal is to contain 95 percent of all wildfires to 10 acres or less. This  

[13:2:66,510] is achieved through detection, ground attack, air attack and mutual aid using fire engines, fire crews, bulldozers, helicopters,  

[13:3:66,520] and fixed wing aircraft.  

[14:0:66,538] 2465028 - Cooperative Fire Protection:  

[15:0:66,555] CAL FIRE provides a full range of fire protection services to local, county, state, and federal agencies throughout California  

[15:1:66,566] through the administration of over 100 cooperative government agreements. Additionally, there are six long standing  

[15:2:66,576] agreements wherein counties provide wildland fire protection on behalf of CAL FIRE.  

[16:0:66,593] 2465037 - Conservation Camps:  

[17:0:66,614] CAL FIRE, in cooperation with the Department of Corrections and Rehabilitation, operates 39 conservation camps throughout  

[17:1:66,624] the state that house 196 fire crews. CAL FIRE also operates four Fire Centers with the California Conservation Corps  

[17:2:66,634] throughout the state that house 12 fire crews. These crews provide one of the primary labor forces for firefighting, emergency  

[17:3:66,644] response, and conservation related work projects.  

[18:0:66,662] 2465046 - Emergency Fire Suppression:  

[19:0:66,683] CAL FIRE incurs additional emergency fire suppression expenditures when budgeted initial attack forces are unable to cope  

[19:1:66,693] with a wildland fire within the initial attack period. Portions of these costs may be reimbursed in the fiscal year the expense was  

[19:2:66,703] incurred or in a future fiscal year via Fire Management Assistance Grants provided by the Federal Emergency Management  

[20:0:66,736] * Dollars in thousands, except in Salary Range. Numbers may not add or match to other statements due to rounding of budget details.

[4:0:66,111] 2470 - RESOURCE MANAGEMENT  

[5:0:66,131] California's state and private forest, range, watershed lands, and urban forests provide multiple human, climate, and  

[5:1:66,141] environmental benefits. The objective of this program is to maintain and enhance those benefits and to minimize damage to  

[5:2:66,152] these resources from natural catastrophes and human development. Objectives are met by the regulation of timber harvesting,  

[5:3:66,162] coordination of climate related activities for the forest sector, technical assistance to non-industrial landowners, operation of  

[5:4:66,172] state demonstration forests, operation of forest nurseries, vegetation management projects, and administration of federal  

[5:5:66,182] forestry assistance programs.  

[6:0:66,200] 2470010 - Resources Protection and Improvement:  

[7:0:66,218] The objective of this program is to improve forest lands and urban forests. Activities include: (1) pest detection, evaluation, and  

[7:1:66,228] management; (2) growing and selling tree seedlings for reforestation, carbon sequestration, and soil erosion control; (3)  

[7:2:66,238] maintenance of a native conifer seed bank; (4) advice and assistance to non-industrial forest landowners on the management  

[7:3:66,248] of forests and improved harvesting and conservation practices; (5) research on and demonstration of sustainable forestry in  

[7:4:66,258] state forests; (6) implementation of the California Forest Improvement Act of 1978; (7) technical assistance and grants to foster  

[7:5:66,269] the environmental, social, and economic benefits of urban forests consistent with the California Urban Forestry Act of 1978;  

[7:6:66,279] and (8) purchasing and monitoring working forest conservation easements per the California Forest Legacy Act of 2008. In the  

[7:7:66,289] area of vegetation management, assistance is provided to public and private landowners to achieve land use objectives by  

[7:8:66,299] reducing damage from wildland fires, increasing wildlife habitat, increasing productivity of forest and rangelands, improving  

[7:9:66,309] water yields and air quality, reducing carbon emissions from wildland fires, increasing firefighter safety, and maintaining  

[7:10:66,320] desirable ecosystems.  

[8:0:66,337] CAL FIRE cooperates with federal, state, and local agencies; local organizations; and private property owners to develop and  

[8:1:66,347] achieve land use objectives. Activities include removal, rearrangement, conversion, or improvement of vegetation using various  

[8:2:66,358] treatment measures such as prescribed fire and mechanical, manual, and biological methods. The Environmental Protection  

[8:3:66,368] Program provides in-house expertise and oversight of the Department's compliance with the California Environmental Quality  

[8:4:66,378] Act, the National Environmental Policy Act, and other environmental laws and regulations applicable to departmental actions.  

[9:0:66,396] 2470019 - Forest Practice Regulations:  

[10:0:66,413] The objective of this program is to maximize sustained production of high-quality timber products while protecting soil, water,  

[10:1:66,424] wildlife, recreation, and other values associated with forested lands. Forest Practice activities include the review and permitting  

[10:2:66,434] of commercial timber harvesting operations on nonfederal timberlands, enforcing state forest practice laws and regulations, and  

[10:3:66,444] monitoring compliance and the effects of timber operations on forest productivity, water quality, watersheds, soils, botanical  

[10:4:66,454] resources, wildlife, fisheries, greenhouse gas emissions, and other environmentally sensitive resources. The Program is also  

[10:5:66,464] responsible for issuing licenses to timber operators (LTOs), providing LTO training and education, and assisting the Board of  

[10:6:66,475] Equalization-Timber Tax Division in their administration of taxes on timber and timberland. 

[11:0:66,492] 2470028 - Forest Resource Inventory and Assessment:  

[12:0:66,513] The objective of this program is to perform a periodic assessment of California's forest and rangeland resources. Activities  

[12:1:66,523] include maintaining and distributing mission-critical data sets for fire planning, emergency incident support, and meeting certain  

[12:2:66,533] regulatory requirements. In addition, the program provides extensive technical and public information regarding statewide fire  

[12:3:66,543] threats, fire hazards, watersheds, various environmental indicators, and urban forestry.  

[13:0:66,561] 2470037 - Professional Foresters Registration Program:  

[14:0:66,581] The Office of Professional Foresters Registration is a statutorily authorized program within the State Board of Forestry and Fire  

[14:1:66,592] Protection. The program is responsible for the licensing and regulation of all persons who practice forestry on non-federal,  

[14:2:66,602] state, and private lands in California pursuant to the Professional Foresters Law of 1972. The program also licenses “certified  

[14:3:66,612] specialties” of forestry, with the single certified specialty currently being Certified Rangeland Managers.  

[15:0:66,630] 2475 - BOARD OF FORESTRY AND FIRE PROTECTION  

[16:0:66,650] The Board of Forestry and Fire Protection is a Governor-appointed body within CAL FIRE. The Board is responsible for  

[16:1:66,660] developing the general forest policy of the state, determining the guidance policies of CAL FIRE, and representing the state's  

[16:2:66,671] interest in federal forests in California. Together, the Board and CAL FIRE work to carry out statutory mandates to protect and  

[16:3:66,681] enhance the state's unique forest and wildland resources. The Board's statutory responsibilities are to:  

[17:0:65,698] • Establish and administer forest and rangeland policy for the State of California;  

[18:0:66,736] * Dollars in thousands, except in Salary Range. Numbers may not add or match to other statements due to rounding of budget details.

[5:2:66,197] 9900100 - ADMINISTRATION  

[6:0:66,218] Departmental administration provides executive leadership, policy direction, and a variety of program support services

[8:0:215,483] 3560   State Lands Commission  

[9:0:66,504] The California State Lands Commission provides effective stewardship of the lands, waterways, and resources entrusted to its  

[9:1:66,514] care through preservation, restoration, enhancement, responsible economic development, and the promotion of public access.  

[9:2:66,525] Execution of the Commission's responsibilities since its inception in 1938 has resulted in the generation of billions of dollars   in  

[9:3:66,535] revenues, while protecting and enhancing the public's ability to enjoy these state lands and resources.  

[10:0:66,552] Consisting of the Lieutenant Governor, the State Controller, and the Governor's Director of Finance, the California State Lands  

[10:1:66,563] Commission serves as a trustee for the people of the state, managing approximately 4 million acres of California's sovereign  

[10:2:66,573] public trust lands and resources, received upon admission into the Union in 1850. It also manages other lands that the federal  

[10:3:66,583] government conveyed to the state (commonly known as school lands) and oversees the management of public trust lands  

[10:4:66,593] legislatively granted in trust to over 70 local jurisdictions. These grants encourage responsible development, environmental  

[10:5:66,603] preservation and enhancement, and public access on the state's tidelands, consistent with the common law Public Trust  

[10:6:66,614] Doctrine. The grants typically require grantees to reinvest revenues generated from the granted lands back into the trust.  

[11:0:66,631] Public trust, or sovereign lands, include the beds of natural and navigable waterways, including non-tidal rivers, streams and  

[11:1:66,642] lakes, and tide and submerged lands within tidal rivers, sloughs, bays, and the Pacific Ocean extending from the ordinary high  

[11:2:66,652] water mark seaward to the three-mile offshore limit. In addition to sovereign lands, the Commission manages school lands,  

[11:3:66,662] which were granted to California by the federal government in 1853 for the purpose of supporting public education in California.  

[11:4:66,672] School lands typically include the 16th and 36th sections of each township and lands known to be mineral in character. Of the  

[11:5:66,682] five and one-half million acres of school lands originally granted to the state, only about 458,843 acres remain in state  

[11:6:66,693] ownership, and these are mostly concentrated in the California desert. The Commission also retains a reserved mineral interest  

[11:7:66,703] in approximately 790,000 acres of sold school lands.  

[12:0:66,736] * Dollars in thousands, except in Salary Range. Numbers may not add or match to other statements due to rounding of budget details.

[7:0:66,411] PROGRAM DESCRIPTIONS  

[8:0:66,431] 2560 - MINERAL RESOURCES MANAGEMENT DIVISION  

[9:0:66,448] The   Mineral Resources Management Division   oversees efficient and safe development of mineral resources on state lands.  

[9:1:66,458] The state also has a beneficial interest in the Long Beach tidelands oil fields, and as such, the Commission participates in the  

[9:2:66,469] safety and fiscal oversight of these fields. The program objectives are to: manage the responsible extraction of oil, gas,  

[9:3:66,479] geothermal resources and other solid minerals; collect royalty and other revenues generated therefrom; and ensure that public  

[9:4:66,489] safety and environmental protection are maintained at the highest possible standards in the development of these resources  

[9:5:66,499] through engineering review and safety audits. The Commission also promulgates regulations for the prevention of oil spills and  

[9:6:66,509] the safe operation of offshore facilities and   conducts program and financial audits to ensure that the state receives fair value for  

[9:7:66,520] the development of its resources. The Commission also manages the safe and effective decommissioning of the oil and gas  

[9:8:66,530] facilities under its jurisdiction.  

[10:0:66,548] Further, the Commission manages the mineral resources associated with school lands under its purview, including revenue-

[10:1:66,558] generating extraction and prospecting leases and other projects, and also coordinates with other agencies to administer the  

[10:2:66,568] statewide Abandoned Mines program.  

[10:3:66,586] 2565 - LAND MANAGEMENT DIVISION  

[11:0:66,603] The Land Management Division manages approximately 4 million acres of tide and submerged lands and the beds of natural  

[11:1:66,614] and navigable rivers, streams, lakes, bays, estuaries, inlets, and straits. These lands, often referred to as sovereign or Public  

[11:2:66,624] Trust lands, stretch from the Klamath River and Goose Lake in the north to the Tijuana Estuary in the south, and from   Lake  

[11:3:66,634] Tahoe and   the Colorado River in the east, to three miles offshore from the Pacific Coast   in the west, and include California’s  

[11:4:66,644] two longest rivers, the Sacramento and San Joaquin.  

[12:0:66,662] Under the Land Management Program, the Commission also monitors sovereign land granted in trust by the California  

[12:1:66,672] Legislature to approximately 70 local jurisdictions that generally consist of prime waterfront lands and coastal waters, including  

[12:2:66,682] the state’s five major ports. The Commission protects and enhances these lands and natural resources by issuing leases for  

[12:3:66,692] use or development, preserving and expanding public access, and resolving boundaries between public and private lands.  

[13:0:66,736] * Dollars in thousands, except in Salary Range. Numbers may not add or match to other statements due to rounding of budget details.

# === PAGE 93 === [size: 612x792]
[0:0:66,13] NATURAL RESOURCES 

[1:0:412,13] 2020-21 STATE BUDGET — RES   91 

[2:0:180,29] 3560   State Lands Commission - Continued 

[3:0:66,83] Finally, the Commission administers the state’s school lands, which seeks to develop and consolidate the state’s school-land  

[3:1:66,93] holdings to the economic benefit of the California State Teachers’ Retirement System.  

[3:2:66,111] 2570 - MARINE ENVIRONMENTAL PROTECTION DIVISION  

[4:0:66,128] The   Marine Environmental Protection Division   adopts rules, regulations, and guidelines for marine oil terminals within  

[4:1:66,139] California, providing the best achievable protection of the public health and safety and the environment. The Division monitors  

[4:2:66,149] the transfer of oil at marine terminals, inspects all marine facilities, and reviews and approves all marine oil terminal operations  

[4:3:66,159] manuals and training and certification programs. The Division also administers Chapter 31F of the California Building Code to  

[4:4:66,169] ensure that marine oil terminals are fit for their purpose, and implements the state's Marine Invasive Species Program to  

[4:5:66,179] prevent the introduction of nonindigenous species into California waters through the operations of oceangoing vessels. The  

[4:6:66,190] program funds and conducts scientific research, develops rules and regulations to implement the Marine Invasive Species Act,  

[4:7:66,200] and physically inspects oceangoing vessels to assess compliance with vessel-reported nonindigenous species management  

[4:8:66,210] actions.  

[5:0:66,228] 9900100 - EXECUTIVE AND ADMINISTRATION  

[6:0:66,245] The Executive and Administration program provides management, policy direction, and administrative support to the  

[6:1:66,256] Commission’s line programs.

[3:0:66,83] MAJOR PROGRAM CHANGES  

[3:1:65,102] • Advancing Biodiversity Protection, Operational Modernization, and Regulatory Efficiencies—The Budget includes a one-time  

[3:2:76,113] augmentation of $6.2 million General Fund in 2020-21 to support land management and a cutting green tape initiative and  

[3:3:76,123] $23.4 million General Fund ongoing starting in 2021-22 to permanently extend the limited-term funding that was first  

[3:4:76,133] provided in the 2018 Budget Act, to allow the Department to continue critical programs.

[3:6:76,159] Cannabis Tax Fund and $4.2 million Fish and Game Preservation Fund) in 2020-21 for three years, and $4.7 million  

[3:7:76,170] Cannabis Tax Fund ongoing for the extension of previously authorized expiring resources to continue implementation of the  

[3:8:76,180] cannabis regulatory program, cannabis cultivation environmental impact permitting, and cannabis law enforcement support.

[5:0:66,194] PROGRAM DESCRIPTIONS  

[6:0:66,213] 2590 - BIODIVERSITY CONSERVATION PROGRAM  

[7:0:66,234] This program encourages the preservation, conservation, maintenance, and restoration of wildlife resources, including the  

[7:1:66,244] Ecosystem Restoration Program, under the jurisdiction and influence of the state. Activities involve the conservation, protection  

[7:2:66,254] and management of fish, wildlife, native plants, and habitat to ensure maintenance of biologically sustainable populations of  

[7:3:66,264] those species.  

[8:0:66,282] 2595 - HUNTING, FISHING, AND PUBLIC USE PROGRAM  

[9:0:66,300] This program facilitates diverse and sustainable hunting, fishing (recreational and commercial), trapping, and other public uses  

[9:1:66,310] and associated economic benefits to the state by conserving and managing game species. Activities include collection and  

[9:2:66,320] assessment of information on the distribution and abundance of game fish and wildlife to determine appropriate regulations  

[9:3:66,330] (bag limits, gear restrictions, etc.) and to monitor the effects of those regulations.  

[10:0:66,348] 2600 - MANAGEMENT OF DEPARTMENT LANDS AND FACILITIES PROGRAM  

[11:0:66,366] This program manages Department-owned or leased lands and facilities, including hatcheries, wildlife areas, ecological  

[11:1:66,376] reserves, fish and wildlife laboratories, and public access areas, to contribute to the conservation, protection, and management  

[11:2:66,386] of fish and wildlife.  

[12:0:66,404] 2605 - ENFORCEMENT  

[13:0:66,424] This program serves the public through law enforcement, public safety and hunter education. Law enforcement promotes  

[13:1:66,434] compliance with laws and regulations protecting fish and wildlife resources; and investigates habitat destruction, pollution  

[13:2:66,445] incidents and illegal commercialization of wildlife. Wardens also serve the public through general law enforcement, mutual aid  

[13:3:66,455] and homeland security.  

[14:0:66,473] 2610 - COMMUNICATIONS, EDUCATION, AND OUTREACH PROGRAM  

[15:0:66,493] This program provides resource conservation education and activities in the classroom and on public and private lands;  

[15:1:66,503] community and stakeholder outreach; and information and data using a variety of methods including publications,  

[15:2:66,513] presentations, web applications, and media relations.  

[16:0:66,531] 2615 - SPILL PREVENTION AND RESPONSE PROGRAM  

[17:0:66,551] This program minimizes damage and environmental impacts to, restores, and rehabilitates California's fish and wildlife  

[17:1:66,562] populations and their habitats from the harmful effects of oil and other deleterious material spills in marine waters and inland  

[17:2:66,572] habitats.  

[18:0:66,590] 2620 - FISH AND GAME COMMISSION  

[19:0:66,607] The California Fish and Game Commission ensures the long term sustainability of California's fish and wildlife resources by  

[19:1:66,617] guiding the ongoing scientific evaluation and assessment of California's fish and wildlife resources; setting California's fish and  

[19:2:66,628] wildlife resource management policies and ensuring these are implemented by the Department of Fish and Wildlife;  

[19:3:66,638] establishing appropriate fish and wildlife resource management rules and regulations; and building active fish and wildlife  

[19:4:66,648] resource management partnerships with individual landowners, the public and interest groups, and federal, state and local  

[19:5:66,658] resource management agencies.  

[20:0:66,736] * Dollars in thousands, except in Salary Range. Numbers may not add or match to other statements due to rounding of budget details.

[8:3:66,686] agencies purchase land for the purpose of habitat or wildlife protection, and management responsibilities for these properties  

[8:4:66,696] are often transferred to the Department; therefore, the amount of land under Department control continues to increase. The

[7:0:66,423] PROGRAM DESCRIPTIONS  

[7:1:66,442] 2710 - WILDLIFE CONSERVATION BOARD  

[8:0:66,460] The Wildlife Conservation Board's three main functions are: (1) land acquisition; (2) habitat protection, restoration, and  

[8:1:66,470] enhancement, including conservation of forests, wetlands, riparian habitat, oak woodlands and protection of rangeland, grazing  

[8:2:66,480] land and grasslands to support biodiversity and climate change adaptation; and (3) development of wildlife-oriented public  

[8:3:66,490] access and recreational areas.

[8:0:198,423] 3720   California Coastal Commission  

[9:0:66,445] The California Coastal Commission, comprised of 12 voting members appointed by the Governor, the Senate Rules  

[9:1:66,455] Committee, and the Speaker of the Assembly, was created by voter initiative in 1972 and was made permanent by the  

[9:2:66,465] California Coastal Act of 1976 (Coastal Act). The Coastal Act calls for the protection and enhancement of public access and  

[9:3:66,475] recreation, marine resources, environmentally sensitive habitat areas, marine water quality, agriculture, and scenic resources;  

[9:4:66,485] and makes provisions for coastal-dependent industrial and energy development. The Commission is also designated the  

[9:5:66,496] principal state coastal management agency for the purpose of administering the federal Coastal Zone Management Act in  

[9:6:66,506] California and has exclusive regulatory authority over federal activities such as permits, leases, federal development projects,  

[9:7:66,516] and other federal actions that could affect coastal zone resources and that would not otherwise be subject to state control.

[5:0:66,155] PROGRAM DESCRIPTIONS  

[5:1:66,174] 2730 - COASTAL MANAGEMENT PROGRAM  

[6:0:66,194] The objectives of the Coastal Management Program are to implement coastal resources conservation through planning and  

[6:1:66,205] regulation. Activities include:  

[7:0:65,222] • Reviewing and approval of local coastal programs (LCPs), port master plans, university long-range development plans, and  

[7:1:76,233] any amendments to such plans, for consistency with the Coastal Act.  

[8:0:65,249] • Reviewing coastal development permit applications for new development in areas without a certified LCP, areas of  

[8:1:76,259] permanently retained jurisdiction (e.g., tidelands, submerged lands, and public trust lands) and limited categories of local  

[8:2:76,269] coastal development permit actions that can be appealed to the Commission.  

[8:3:65,285] • Monitoring and enforcement of coastal development permits.  

[8:4:65,302] • Reviewing federal activities for consistency with the Coastal Act.  

[8:5:65,318] • Protecting and expanding opportunities for public coastal access and recreation.  

[8:6:65,334] • Implementing a coastal water quality protection program.  

[8:7:65,350] • Providing technical information and assistance to support effective coastal management.  

[8:8:65,366] • Implementing a coastal and ocean resource public education program.  

[9:0:65,383] • Addressing coastal energy issues including, but not limited to, offshore oil and gas development, offshore wind projects,  

[9:1:76,393] electricity generating power plant expansion and development, and siting and development of liquefied natural gas facilities.  

[9:2:65,409] • Providing administrative support including accounting, budgeting, business services, support services, information  

[9:3:76,419] technology, and human resources services.

[9:0:210,473] 3760   State Coastal Conservancy  

[10:0:66,494] The State Coastal Conservancy develops and implements projects to protect, restore and enhance natural, recreational, and  

[10:1:66,504] economic resources along California's coast, coastal watersheds, the ocean, and within the San Francisco Bay Area. The  

[10:2:66,515] Coastal Conservancy supports implementation of the San Francisco Bay Area Conservancy Program and the Santa Ana River  

[10:3:66,525] Conservancy Program. Through technical assistance, direct funding, and grants to local agencies and nonprofit organizations,  

[10:4:66,535] the Coastal Conservancy supports projects that:  

[11:0:65,553] • Protect and enhance the natural environment, agricultural lands, scenic lands and urban waterfronts along the Coast and  

[11:1:76,563] around San Francisco Bay.  

[11:2:65,579] • Increase public access to the coast and San Francisco Bay.  

[11:3:65,595] • Build trails and increase outdoor recreational opportunities.  

[11:4:65,612] • Enhance coastal wetlands and other important habitat areas.  

[12:0:65,628] • Prepare for and reduce the unavoidable impacts of climate change on the coastal region and San Francisco Bay Area.  

[12:1:65,644] • Support education projects and programs related to the preservation, enhancement, and enjoyment of coastal resources.  

[12:2:65,660] • Support education programs about coastal resources for kindergarten through grade 12.  

[13:0:66,736] * Dollars in thousands, except in Salary Range. Numbers may not add or match to other statements due to rounding of budget details.

[8:0:66,482] PROGRAM DESCRIPTIONS  

[8:1:66,502] 2790 - COASTAL CONSERVANCY PROGRAM  

[8:2:66,519] The Coastal Conservancy seeks to achieve the following goals:  

[9:0:65,537] • Protect and enhance habitats, wetlands, scenic open space, working lands and urban waterfronts along the California coast  

[9:1:76,547] and around San Francisco Bay.  

[10:0:65,564] • Increase public access to the coast and outdoor recreational opportunities by creating new parks, trails and recreational  

[10:1:76,574] amenities.  

[11:0:65,590] • Prepare for and reduce the unavoidable impacts of climate change on the coastal region and San Francisco Bay Area.  

[11:1:65,606] • Support education projects and programs related to the preservation, enhancement, and enjoyment of coastal resources  

[11:2:76,616] Facilitate improvement of existing, and the development of new, lower cost accommodations within one and one-half miles of  

[11:3:76,627] the coast.  

[12:0:66,644] 2805 - STATE COASTAL CONSERVANCY LOCAL ASSISTANCE PROGRAM  

[13:0:66,665] The State Coastal Conservancy provides grants and technical assistance to local governments, nonprofits and other project  

[13:1:66,675] partners for the acquisition of land and easements, the development of public facilities, and the restoration of habitat. The State  

[13:2:66,685] Coastal Conservancy helps to implement the State's federally approved Coastal Management Program and supports programs  

[13:3:66,695] that increase public access to the coast. The Conservancy has participated in the protection of more than 390,000 acres of  

[13:4:66,705] coastal lands through acquisition of fee title and conservation easements. The Conservancy has helped restore more than  

[13:5:66,716] 33,000 acres of habitat.  

[14:0:66,736] * Dollars in thousands, except in Salary Range. Numbers may not add or match to other statements due to rounding of budget details.

[6:2:66,192] implement the State's federally approved Coastal Management Program. The Conservancy has participated in the protection of  

[6:3:66,203] more than 390,000 acres of coastal lands through acquisition of fee title and conservation easements. The Conservancy has  

[6:4:66,213] helped restore more than 33,000 acres of habitat.

[8:0:183,330] 3790   Department of Parks and Recreation  

[9:0:66,351] The mission of the California Department of Parks and Recreation (Parks) is to provide for the health, inspiration, and  

[9:1:66,361] education of the people of California by helping to preserve the state's extraordinary biological diversity, protecting its most  

[9:2:66,372] valued natural, cultural and historical resources, and creating opportunities for high-quality outdoor recreation for current and  

[9:3:66,382] future generations to enjoy. With increased urbanization, the establishment of park units and recreation areas accessible to the  

[9:4:66,392] major population centers of the state has become particularly important. Specific activities include: stewardship of natural  

[9:5:66,402] resources, historic, cultural and archeological sites, and artifacts and structures; provision of interpretive services for park  

[9:6:66,412] visitors; construction and maintenance of campsites, trails, visitor centers, museums, and infrastructure such as roads and  

[9:7:66,423] water systems; and creation of recreational opportunities such as hiking, bicycling, fishing, swimming, horseback riding,  

[9:8:66,433] jogging, camping, picnicking, and off-highway vehicle recreation. In addition, the Division of Boating and Waterways funds,  

[9:9:66,443] plans, and develops boating facilities on waterways throughout California and ensures safe boating for the public by providing  

[9:10:66,453] financial aid and training to local law enforcement agencies.  

[10:0:66,474] Because Parks' programs drive a need for infrastructure investment, Parks has a capital outlay program to support this need.  

[10:1:66,484] For the specifics on Parks' capital outlay program see "Infrastructure Overview."

[10:0:177,400] 3810   Santa Monica Mountains Conservancy  

[11:0:66,422] The Santa Monica Mountains Conservancy acquires, restores, and consolidates lands in the Santa Monica Mountains, Rim of  

[11:1:66,432] the Valley Trail Corridor, Upper Los Angeles River Watershed, and watersheds of Santa Monica Bay for park, recreation, and  

[11:2:66,442] conservation purposes. The Conservancy may:  

[11:3:65,460] • Lease, rent, sell, transfer, or exchange lands for park purposes.  

[12:0:65,476] • Award grants or interest free loans to state and local agencies to purchase or restore park, recreation, conservation, or  

[12:1:76,486] buffer-zone areas to ensure that the character and intensity of development on these lands is generally compatible with and  

[12:2:76,496] does not adversely impact the environment.  

[13:0:65,513] • Implement programs to improve access from the inner city areas surrounding the Conservancy, thereby providing  

[13:1:76,523] recreational opportunities for all residents.  

[13:2:65,539] • Manage the Rim of the Valley Trail Corridor recreational trail.  

[13:3:65,555] • Plans for restoration, recreation, and conservation projects for the Los Angeles River and Upper Los Angeles River  

[13:4:76,565] Watershed.

[9:0:104,614] 3820   San Francisco Bay Conservation and Development Commission  

[10:0:66,635] The San Francisco Bay Conservation and Development Commission is a state planning and regulatory agency with regional  

[10:1:66,645] authority. Its mission is to protect and enhance the   San Francisco Bay, and to encourage the Bay's responsible and productive  

[10:2:66,655] use. The Commission authored and maintains the San Francisco Bay Plan, and relies on the McAteer-Petris Act and other  

[10:3:66,666] statutory and regulatory authorities to maximize public access to the Bay and minimize Bay fill. The Commission issues permits  

[10:4:66,676] for filling, dredging, and development projects within the Bay, along the Bay shoreline, and within salt ponds and certain  

[10:5:66,686] managed wetlands adjacent to the Bay. The Commission also implements the Suisun Marsh Preservation Act of 1977. By  

[10:6:66,696] statute, the Commission develops and implements the federal Coastal Zone Management Act's program for the Bay and

[9:0:81,453] 3825   San Gabriel and Lower Los Angeles Rivers and Mountains Conservancy  

[9:1:66,475] The San Gabriel and Lower Los Angeles Rivers and Mountains Conservancy was established in 1999 to acquire and manage  

[9:2:66,485] public lands. Through these acquisitions, the Conservancy provides for additional open space, low-impact recreational uses,  

[9:3:66,495] educational uses, water conservation and watershed improvement, and wildlife and habitat restoration and protection. These  

[9:4:66,506] acquisitions also help to preserve the San Gabriel Mountains and the San Gabriel and Lower Los Angeles Rivers and their  

[9:5:66,516] tributaries, consistent with existing and adopted river and flood control projects for the protection of life and property.

[9:0:195,510] 3830   San Joaquin River Conservancy  

[9:1:66,532] The mission of the San Joaquin River Conservancy is to acquire, preserve, manage, and promote access to lands within the  

[9:2:66,542] flood plain on both sides of the San Joaquin River from Friant Dam to Highway 99.  

[9:3:66,560] Specific activities are to:  

[10:0:65,577] • Implement the San Joaquin River Parkway Master Plan, a 22-mile regional greenspace and wildlife corridor along both sides  

[10:1:76,588] of the river extending from Friant Dam to Highway 99, with an interconnected trail system and recreational and educational  

[10:2:76,598] features.  

[11:0:65,614] • Operate and manage lands for public access, recreation, and enjoyment consistent with the protection of natural and cultural  

[11:1:76,624] resources.  

[12:0:65,640] • Protect, enhance, and restore riparian and riverine habitat and ecological diversity.  

[12:1:65,657] • Provide for multi-benefit water quality, water supply, ecosystem and watershed protection and restoration.  

[12:2:65,673] • Facilitate the development of the parkway by assisting other entities in conserving and improving their lands within the  

[12:3:76,683] parkway, and to garner public support to secure its future.  

[12:4:65,699] • Acquire land from willing sellers to facilitate the above activities with a target of 5,900 acres.  

[13:0:66,736] * Dollars in thousands, except in Salary Range. Numbers may not add or match to other statements due to rounding of budget details.

[7:0:210,366] 3835   Baldwin Hills Conservancy  

[7:1:66,388] The Baldwin Hills Conservancy was established in 2000 to acquire open space and manage public lands within the Baldwin  

[7:2:66,398] Hills area and to provide recreation, restoration, and protection of wildlife habitat for the public's enjoyment and educational  

[7:3:66,408] experience.  

[8:0:66,428] Specific agency responsibilities are to:  

[8:1:65,446] • Approve the Baldwin Hills Park Master Plan.  

[9:0:65,462] • Undertake site improvement projects, regulate public access, construct new facilities as needed for outdoor recreation, and  

[9:1:76,472] provide for the appreciation and protection of natural resources.  

[9:2:65,489] • Prioritize and implement acquisition of additional recreational and open space land within the Baldwin Hills area.  

[9:3:65,505] • Provide recreational, open space, wildlife habitat restoration and protection of lands for educational use within the area.  

[9:4:65,521] • Conduct the necessary planning activities for the area.  

[10:0:65,537] • Approve conservancy funded projects that advance the policies and priorities set forth by the enabling statute.  

[10:1:65,553] • Develop and coordinate a program of resource stewardship for optimum recreational and natural resource value based on  

[10:2:76,564] the needs of the surrounding community.  

[11:0:65,580] • Provide for the public's enjoyment, and enhance recreational and educational experiences on public lands in a manner  

[11:1:76,590] consistent with the protection of lands and resources in the area.

[11:0:203,588] 3840   Delta Protection Commission  

[12:0:66,610] The mission of the Delta Protection Commission is to protect, maintain, enhance, and enrich the overall quality of the Delta  

[12:1:66,620] environment and economy, with a focus on agriculture, recreation, and natural resources,   while remaining   mindful of the  

[12:2:66,630] importance of the Delta to all Californians. The Commission ensures orderly, balanced conservation and development of Delta  

[12:3:66,640] land resources and provides a forum for Delta residents to engage in decisions regarding actions to recognize and enhance the  

[12:4:66,650] unique cultural, recreational, and agricultural resources of the Delta. The Commission identifies and makes recommendations  

[12:5:66,661] on methods of preserving the Delta as an evolving place and promotes Delta legacy communities, regional economic  

[12:6:66,671] sustainability, and emergency response preparedness.  

[13:0:66,736] * Dollars in thousands, except in Salary Range. Numbers may not add or match to other statements due to rounding of budget details.

[13:1:66,649] emphasizing Land Conservation, Recreation and Education, Natural and Cultural Resources, Water Quality and Natural Flood  

[13:2:66,659] Conveyance, Organizational Capacity and Reach, and the San Diego Rivers Watershed Consortium. This mission will be  

[13:3:66,669] accomplished in part by building with our partners the San Diego River Park and hiking trail stretching over 52 miles from the  

[13:4:66,679] headwaters in Julian to the Pacific Ocean. The Conservancy has established advisory panels to assist in restoring and  

[13:5:66,689] enhancing three nearby watersheds: the Otay River, Sweetwater River and Tijuana River. Working with stakeholders within  

[13:6:66,700] these watersheds foster collaboration among governments, public agencies and nonprofit organizations, and leverages grant  

[13:7:66,710] funding to achieve an array of conservation objectives for the region with an emphasis on native habitat restoration and
